<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hayes-inaugurations.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A large crowd of people outside the United States Capitol building" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Hayes-inaugurations.jpg/220px-Hayes-inaugurations.jpg" width="220" height="281" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Hayes-inaugurations.jpg/330px-Hayes-inaugurations.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Hayes-inaugurations.jpg/440px-Hayes-inaugurations.jpg 2x" data-file-width="470" data-file-height="601" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hayes-inaugurations.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Chief Justice <a href="/wiki/Morrison_R._Waite" title="Morrison R. Waite" class="mw-redirect">Morrison R. Waite</a> administering the oath of office to Hayes</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Because March 4, 1877 fell on a Sunday, Hayes took the oath of office privately on Saturday, March 3, in the <a href="/wiki/Red_Room_(White_House)" title="Red Room (White House)">Red Room</a> of the <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">White House</a>, the first president to do so in the Executive Mansion. He took the oath publicly on the following Monday on the East Portico of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Capitol" title="United States Capitol">United States Capitol</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom295.E2.80.93297_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom295.E2.80.93297-119"><span>[</span>115<span>]</span></a></sup> In his inaugural address, Hayes attempted to soothe the passions of the past few months, saying that "he serves his party best who serves his country best".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrefousse85.E2.80.9386_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrefousse85.E2.80.9386-120"><span>[</span>116<span>]</span></a></sup> He pledged to support "wise, honest, and peaceful local self-government" in the South, as well as reform of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_civil_service" title="United States civil service" class="mw-redirect">civil service</a> and a full return to the <a href="/wiki/Gold_standard" title="Gold standard">gold standard</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom298.E2.80.93299_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom298.E2.80.93299-121"><span>[</span>117<span>]</span></a></sup> Despite his message of conciliation, many Democrats never considered Hayes's election legitimate and referred to him as "Rutherfraud" or "His Fraudulency" for the next four years.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarnard402.E2.80.93403_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarnard402.E2.80.93403-122"><span>[</span>118<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_South_and_the_end_of_Reconstruction">The South and the end of Reconstruction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rutherford_B._Hayes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: The South and the end of Reconstruction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Hayes had been a firm supporter of Republican <a href="/wiki/Reconstruction_era_of_the_United_States" title="Reconstruction era of the United States" class="mw-redirect">Reconstruction</a> policies throughout his political career, but the first major act of his presidency was an end to Reconstruction and the return of the South to "home rule".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrefousse90.E2.80.9393_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrefousse90.E2.80.9393-123"><span>[</span>119<span>]</span></a></sup> Even without the conditions of the Wormley's Hotel agreement, Hayes would have been hard-pressed to continue the policies of his predecessors. The House of Representatives in the <a href="/wiki/45th_United_States_Congress" title="45th United States Congress">45th Congress</a> was controlled by a majority of Democrats that refused to appropriate enough funds for the army to continue to garrison the South.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom304.E2.80.93307Foner580.E2.80.93583Davison142_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom304.E2.80.93307Foner580.E2.80.93583Davison142-124"><span>[</span>120<span>]</span></a></sup> Even among Republicans, devotion to continued military Reconstruction was fading in the face of persistent Southern insurgency and violence.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavison138Trefousse92_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavison138Trefousse92-125"><span>[</span>121<span>]</span></a></sup> Only two states were still under Reconstruction's sway when Hayes assumed the Presidency and, without troops to enforce the voting rights laws, these soon fell.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClendenen244_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClendenen244-126"><span>[</span>122<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128"><span>[</span>e<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Hayes's later attempts to protect the rights of southern blacks were ineffective, as were his attempts to rebuild Republican strength in the South.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom317.E2.80.93318Davison141.E2.80.93143_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom317.E2.80.93318Davison141.E2.80.93143-129"><span>[</span>124<span>]</span></a></sup> He did, however, defeat Congress's efforts to curtail federal power to monitor federal elections.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavison162.E2.80.93163Hoogenboom392.E2.80.93402Richardson161_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavison162.E2.80.93163Hoogenboom392.E2.80.93402Richardson161-130"><span>[</span>125<span>]</span></a></sup> Democrats in Congress passed an army <a href="/wiki/Appropriation_bill" title="Appropriation bill">appropriation bill</a> in 1879 with a <a href="/wiki/Rider_(legislation)" title="Rider (legislation)">rider</a> that repealed the <a href="/wiki/Force_Acts" title="Force Acts" class="mw-redirect">Force Acts</a>, which had been used to suppress the <a href="/wiki/Ku_Klux_Klan" title="Ku Klux Klan">Ku Klux Klan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavison162.E2.80.93163Hoogenboom392.E2.80.93402Richardson161_130-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavison162.E2.80.93163Hoogenboom392.E2.80.93402Richardson161-130"><span>[</span>125<span>]</span></a></sup> Those Acts, passed during Reconstruction, made it a crime to prevent someone from voting because of his race. Hayes was determined to preserve the law protecting black voters, and he vetoed the appropriation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavison162.E2.80.93163Hoogenboom392.E2.80.93402Richardson161_130-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavison162.E2.80.93163Hoogenboom392.E2.80.93402Richardson161-130"><span>[</span>125<span>]</span></a></sup> The Democrats did not have enough votes to override the veto, but they passed a new bill with the same rider. Hayes vetoed this as well, and the process was repeated three times more.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavison162.E2.80.93163Hoogenboom392.E2.80.93402Richardson161_130-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavison162.E2.80.93163Hoogenboom392.E2.80.93402Richardson161-130"><span>[</span>125<span>]</span></a></sup> Finally, Hayes signed an appropriation without the offensive rider, but Congress refused to pass another bill to fund federal marshals, who were vital to the enforcement of the Force Acts.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavison162.E2.80.93163Hoogenboom392.E2.80.93402Richardson161_130-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavison162.E2.80.93163Hoogenboom392.E2.80.93402Richardson161-130"><span>[</span>125<span>]</span></a></sup> The election laws remained in effect, but the funds to enforce them were curtailed for the time being.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom402_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom402-131"><span>[</span>126<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Hayes tried to reconcile the social <a href="/wiki/Mores" title="Mores">mores</a> of the South with the recently passed civil rights laws by distributing patronage among southern Democrats. "My task was to wipe out the color line, to abolish sectionalism, to end the war and bring peace," he wrote in his diary. "To do this, I was ready to resort to unusual measures and to risk my own standing and reputation within my party and the country."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarnard418_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarnard418-132"><span>[</span>127<span>]</span></a></sup> All of his efforts were in vain; Hayes failed to convince the South to accept the idea of racial equality and failed to convince Congress to appropriate funds to enforce the <a href="/wiki/Civil_Rights_Act_of_1875" title="Civil Rights Act of 1875">civil rights laws</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom317.E2.80.93318_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom317.E2.80.93318-133"><span>[</span>128<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Civil_service_reform">Civil service reform</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rutherford_B._Hayes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Civil service reform">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Arthur_NYcustoms_house_cartoon.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Cartoon of one man kicking another out of a building" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Arthur_NYcustoms_house_cartoon.jpg/220px-Arthur_NYcustoms_house_cartoon.jpg" width="220" height="178" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Arthur_NYcustoms_house_cartoon.jpg/330px-Arthur_NYcustoms_house_cartoon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Arthur_NYcustoms_house_cartoon.jpg/440px-Arthur_NYcustoms_house_cartoon.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1567" data-file-height="1269" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Arthur_NYcustoms_house_cartoon.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Hayes kicking <a href="/wiki/Chester_A._Arthur" title="Chester A. Arthur">Chester A. Arthur</a> out of the New York Custom House</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Hayes took office determined to reform the system of civil service appointments, which had been based on the <a href="/wiki/Spoils_system" title="Spoils system">spoils system</a> since <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Jackson" title="Andrew Jackson">Andrew Jackson</a> was president.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrefousse93.E2.80.9394_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrefousse93.E2.80.9394-134"><span>[</span>129<span>]</span></a></sup> Instead of giving federal jobs to political supporters, Hayes wished to award them by merit according to an <a href="/wiki/Civil_service_examination" title="Civil service examination">examination</a> that all applicants would take.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom318.E2.80.93319_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom318.E2.80.93319-135"><span>[</span>130<span>]</span></a></sup> Immediately, Hayes's call for reform brought him into conflict with the <a href="/wiki/Stalwart_(politics)" title="Stalwart (politics)">Stalwart</a>, or pro-spoils, branch of the Republican party. Senators of both parties were accustomed to being consulted about political appointments and turned against Hayes. Foremost among his enemies was New York Senator <a href="/wiki/Roscoe_Conkling" title="Roscoe Conkling">Roscoe Conkling</a>, who fought Hayes's reform efforts at every turn.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavison164.E2.80.93165_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavison164.E2.80.93165-136"><span>[</span>131<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>To show his commitment to reform, Hayes appointed one of the best-known advocates of reform, <a href="/wiki/Carl_Schurz" title="Carl Schurz">Carl Schurz</a>, to be <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_the_Interior" title="United States Secretary of the Interior">Secretary of the Interior</a> and asked Schurz and <a href="/wiki/William_M._Evarts" title="William M. Evarts">William M. Evarts</a>, his <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_State" title="United States Secretary of State">Secretary of State</a>, to lead a special cabinet committee charged with drawing up new rules for federal appointments.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPaul71_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPaul71-137"><span>[</span>132<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_Sherman" title="John Sherman">John Sherman</a>, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_the_Treasury" title="United States Secretary of the Treasury">Treasury Secretary</a>, ordered <a href="/wiki/John_Jay_(lawyer)" title="John Jay (lawyer)">John Jay</a> to investigate the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Custom_House" title="New York Custom House" class="mw-redirect">New York Custom House</a>, which was stacked with Conkling's spoilsmen.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom318.E2.80.93319_135-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom318.E2.80.93319-135"><span>[</span>130<span>]</span></a></sup> Jay's report suggested that the New York Custom House was so overstaffed with political appointees that 20% of the employees were expendable.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom322.E2.80.93325Davison164.E2.80.93165Trefousse95.E2.80.9396_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom322.E2.80.93325Davison164.E2.80.93165Trefousse95.E2.80.9396-138"><span>[</span>133<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Although he could not convince Congress to outlaw the spoils system, Hayes issued an <a href="/wiki/Executive_order_(United_States)" title="Executive order (United States)" class="mw-redirect">executive order</a> that forbade federal office holders from being required to make campaign contributions or otherwise taking part in party politics.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom322.E2.80.93325Davison164.E2.80.93165Trefousse95.E2.80.9396_138-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom322.E2.80.93325Davison164.E2.80.93165Trefousse95.E2.80.9396-138"><span>[</span>133<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Chester_A._Arthur" title="Chester A. Arthur">Chester A. Arthur</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Collector_of_the_Port_of_New_York" title="Collector of the Port of New York">Collector of the Port of New York</a>, and his subordinates <a href="/wiki/Alonzo_B._Cornell" title="Alonzo B. Cornell">Alonzo B. Cornell</a> and <a href="/wiki/George_H._Sharpe" title="George H. Sharpe">George H. Sharpe</a>, all Conkling supporters, refused to obey the president's order.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom322.E2.80.93325Davison164.E2.80.93165Trefousse95.E2.80.9396_138-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom322.E2.80.93325Davison164.E2.80.93165Trefousse95.E2.80.9396-138"><span>[</span>133<span>]</span></a></sup> In September 1877, Hayes demanded the three men's resignations, which they refused to give. He submitted appointments of <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Roosevelt,_Sr." title="Theodore Roosevelt, Sr." class="mw-redirect">Theodore Roosevelt, Sr.</a>, <a href="/wiki/L._Bradford_Prince" title="L. Bradford Prince">L. Bradford Prince</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Edwin_Atkins_Merritt" title="Edwin Atkins Merritt">Edwin Merritt</a>—all supporters of Evarts, Conkling's New York rival—to the Senate for confirmation as their replacements.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom352Trefousse95.E2.80.9396_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom352Trefousse95.E2.80.9396-139"><span>[</span>134<span>]</span></a></sup> The Senate's Commerce Committee, which Conkling chaired, voted unanimously to reject the nominees, and the full Senate rejected Roosevelt and Prince by a vote of 31–25, confirming Merritt only because Sharpe's term had expired.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom353.E2.80.93355Trefousse100.E2.80.93101_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom353.E2.80.93355Trefousse100.E2.80.93101-140"><span>[</span>135<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Hayes was forced to wait until July 1878 when, during a Congressional recess, he sacked Arthur and Cornell and replaced them by <a href="/wiki/Recess_appointment" title="Recess appointment">recess appointments</a> of Merritt and <a href="/wiki/Silas_W._Burt" title="Silas W. Burt">Silas W. Burt</a>, respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom370.E2.80.93371_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom370.E2.80.93371-141"><span>[</span>136<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143"><span>[</span>f<span>]</span></a></sup> Conkling opposed the appointees' confirmation when the Senate reconvened in February 1879, but Merritt was approved by a vote of 31–25, as was Burt by 31–19, giving Hayes his most significant civil service reform victory.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom382.E2.80.93384Barnard456_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom382.E2.80.93384Barnard456-144"><span>[</span>138<span>]</span></a></sup> For the remainder of his term, Hayes pressed Congress to enact permanent reform legislation and fund the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Civil_Service_Commission" title="United States Civil Service Commission">United States Civil Service Commission</a>, even using his last <a href="/wiki/State_of_the_Union_address" title="State of the Union address" class="mw-redirect">annual message</a> to Congress in 1880 to appeal for reform. While reform legislation did not pass during Hayes's presidency, his advocacy provided "a significant precedent as well as the political impetus for the <a href="/wiki/Pendleton_Civil_Service_Reform_Act" title="Pendleton Civil Service Reform Act">Pendleton Act</a> of 1883," which was signed into law by President Chester Arthur.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPaul73.E2.80.9374_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPaul73.E2.80.9374-145"><span>[</span>139<span>]</span></a></sup> Hayes allowed some exceptions to the ban on assessments, permitting <a href="/wiki/George_Congdon_Gorham" title="George Congdon Gorham">George Congdon Gorham</a>, secretary of the Republican Congressional Committee, to solicit campaign contributions from federal office-holders during the Congressional elections of 1878.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESproat165-166_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESproat165-166-146"><span>[</span>140<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Hayes also dealt with <a href="/wiki/Star_route_scandal" title="Star route scandal">corruption in the postal service</a>. In 1880, Schurz and Senator <a href="/wiki/John_A._Logan" title="John A. Logan">John A. Logan</a> asked Hayes to shut down the "<a href="/wiki/Star_routes" title="Star routes">star route</a>" rings, a system of corrupt contract profiteering in the Postal Service, and to fire Second Assistant Postmaster-General <a href="/wiki/Thomas_J._Brady" title="Thomas J. Brady">Thomas J. Brady</a>, the alleged ring leader.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKlotsche409.E2.80.93411_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKlotsche409.E2.80.93411-147"><span>[</span>141<span>]</span></a></sup> Hayes stopped granting new star route contracts, but let existing contracts continue to be enforced.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom439.E2.80.93440_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom439.E2.80.93440-148"><span>[</span>142<span>]</span></a></sup> Democrats accused Hayes of delaying proper investigation so as not to injure Republican chances in the 1880 elections but did not press the issue in their campaign literature, as members of both parties were implicated in the corruption.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKlotsche409.E2.80.93411_147-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKlotsche409.E2.80.93411-147"><span>[</span>141<span>]</span></a></sup> As historian <a href="/wiki/Hans_L._Trefousse" title="Hans L. Trefousse">Hans L. Trefousse</a> later wrote, Hayes "hardly knew the chief suspect [Brady] and certainly had no connection with the [star route] corruption."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrefousse144_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrefousse144-149"><span>[</span>143<span>]</span></a></sup> Although Hayes and the Congress both investigated the contracts and found no compelling evidence of wrongdoing, Brady and others were indicted for conspiracy in 1882.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKlotsche414.E2.80.93415_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKlotsche414.E2.80.93415-150"><span>[</span>144<span>]</span></a></sup> After two trials, the defendants were found not guilty in 1883.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKlotsche416_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKlotsche416-151"><span>[</span>145<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Great_Railroad_Strike">Great Railroad Strike</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rutherford_B._Hayes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Great Railroad Strike">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Harpers_8_11_1877_Destruction_of_the_Union_Depot.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A burning building" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Harpers_8_11_1877_Destruction_of_the_Union_Depot.jpg/220px-Harpers_8_11_1877_Destruction_of_the_Union_Depot.jpg" width="220" height="148" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Harpers_8_11_1877_Destruction_of_the_Union_Depot.jpg/330px-Harpers_8_11_1877_Destruction_of_the_Union_Depot.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Harpers_8_11_1877_Destruction_of_the_Union_Depot.jpg/440px-Harpers_8_11_1877_Destruction_of_the_Union_Depot.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2437" data-file-height="1641" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Harpers_8_11_1877_Destruction_of_the_Union_Depot.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Burning of Union Depot, Pittsburgh, Pennsylvania, July 21–22, 1877</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In his first year in office, Hayes was faced with the United States' largest labor disturbance to date, the <a href="/wiki/Great_Railroad_Strike_of_1877" title="Great Railroad Strike of 1877">Great Railroad Strike of 1877</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFoner583Stowell1.E2.80.932Richardson121_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFoner583Stowell1.E2.80.932Richardson121-152"><span>[</span>146<span>]</span></a></sup> In order to make up for financial losses suffered since the panic of 1873, the major railroads cut their employees' wages several times in 1877.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom326.E2.80.93327_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom326.E2.80.93327-153"><span>[</span>147<span>]</span></a></sup> In July of that year, workers from the <a href="/wiki/Baltimore_%26_Ohio_Railroad" title="Baltimore &amp; Ohio Railroad" class="mw-redirect">Baltimore &amp; Ohio Railroad</a> walked off the job in <a href="/wiki/Martinsburg,_West_Virginia" title="Martinsburg, West Virginia">Martinsburg, West Virginia</a>, to protest their reduction in pay.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruce75.E2.80.9377Stowell117_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruce75.E2.80.9377Stowell117-154"><span>[</span>148<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Strike_action" title="Strike action">strike</a> quickly spread to workers of the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Central_Railroad" title="New York Central Railroad">New York Central</a>, <a href="/wiki/Erie_Railroad" title="Erie Railroad">Erie</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_Railroad" title="Pennsylvania Railroad">Pennsylvania</a> railroads, with the strikers soon numbering in the thousands.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom328.E2.80.93333Davison145.E2.80.93153Barnard445.E2.80.93447_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom328.E2.80.93333Davison145.E2.80.93153Barnard445.E2.80.93447-155"><span>[</span>149<span>]</span></a></sup> Fearing a <a href="/wiki/Riot" title="Riot">riot</a>, Governor <a href="/wiki/Henry_M._Mathews" title="Henry M. Mathews">Henry M. Mathews</a> asked Hayes to send federal troops to Martinsburg, and Hayes did so, but when the troops arrived there was no riot, only a peaceful protest.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruce93.E2.80.9394_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruce93.E2.80.9394-156"><span>[</span>150<span>]</span></a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Baltimore" title="Baltimore">Baltimore</a>, however, a riot did erupt on July 20 and Hayes ordered the troops at <a href="/wiki/Fort_McHenry" title="Fort McHenry">Fort McHenry</a> to assist the governor in its suppression; by the time they arrived, the riot had dispersed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom328.E2.80.93333Davison145.E2.80.93153Barnard445.E2.80.93447_155-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom328.E2.80.93333Davison145.E2.80.93153Barnard445.E2.80.93447-155"><span>[</span>149<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pittsburgh" title="Pittsburgh">Pittsburgh</a> next exploded into riots, but Hayes was reluctant to send in troops without the governor first requesting them.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom328.E2.80.93333Davison145.E2.80.93153Barnard445.E2.80.93447_155-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom328.E2.80.93333Davison145.E2.80.93153Barnard445.E2.80.93447-155"><span>[</span>149<span>]</span></a></sup> Other discontented citizens joined the railroad workers in rioting.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStowell116.E2.80.93127Hoogenboom328_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStowell116.E2.80.93127Hoogenboom328-157"><span>[</span>151<span>]</span></a></sup> After a few days, Hayes resolved to send in troops to protect federal property wherever it appeared to be threatened and gave Major General <a href="/wiki/Winfield_Scott_Hancock" title="Winfield Scott Hancock">Winfield Scott Hancock</a> overall command of the situation, marking the first use of federal troops to break a strike against a private company.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom328.E2.80.93333Davison145.E2.80.93153Barnard445.E2.80.93447_155-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom328.E2.80.93333Davison145.E2.80.93153Barnard445.E2.80.93447-155"><span>[</span>149<span>]</span></a></sup> The riot spread further, to <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">Chicago</a> and <a href="/wiki/St._Louis,_Missouri" title="St. Louis, Missouri" class="mw-redirect">St. Louis</a>, where strikers shut down railroad facilities.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom328.E2.80.93333Davison145.E2.80.93153Barnard445.E2.80.93447_155-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom328.E2.80.93333Davison145.E2.80.93153Barnard445.E2.80.93447-155"><span>[</span>149<span>]</span></a></sup> By July 29, the riots had ended and federal troops returned to their barracks.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFoner585_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFoner585-158"><span>[</span>152<span>]</span></a></sup> Although no federal troops had killed any of the strikers, or been killed themselves, clashes between state militia troops and strikers resulted in deaths on both sides.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavison148.E2.80.93150Trefousse95_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavison148.E2.80.93150Trefousse95-159"><span>[</span>153<span>]</span></a></sup> The railroads were victorious in the short term, as the workers returned to their jobs and some wage cuts remained in effect. But, the public blamed the railroads for the strikes and violence, and they were compelled to improve working conditions and make no further cuts.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom334Davison152.E2.80.93153_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom334Davison152.E2.80.93153-160"><span>[</span>154<span>]</span></a></sup> Business leaders praised Hayes, but his own opinion was more equivocal; as he recorded in his diary: "The strikes have been put down by <i>force;</i> but now for the <i>real</i> remedy. Can't something [be] done by education of strikers, by judicious control of capitalists, by wise general policy to end or diminish the evil? The railroad strikers, as a rule, are good men, sober, intelligent, and industrious."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarnard446.E2.80.93447Hayes440v._3_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarnard446.E2.80.93447Hayes440v._3-161"><span>[</span>155<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Currency_debate">Currency debate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rutherford_B._Hayes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Currency debate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:John-Sherman-2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Black-and-white photograph of a man, seated" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/John-Sherman-2.jpg/220px-John-Sherman-2.jpg" width="220" height="265" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/John-Sherman-2.jpg/330px-John-Sherman-2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/5c/John-Sherman-2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="406" data-file-height="489" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:John-Sherman-2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Treasury Secretary <a href="/wiki/John_Sherman" title="John Sherman">John Sherman</a> worked with Hayes to return the country to the gold standard.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Hayes confronted two issues regarding the <a href="/wiki/Currency" title="Currency">currency</a>, the first of which was the <a href="/wiki/Free_Silver" title="Free Silver" class="mw-redirect">coinage of silver</a>, and its relation to <a href="/wiki/Gold_standard" title="Gold standard">gold</a>. In 1873, the <a href="/wiki/Coinage_Act_of_1873" title="Coinage Act of 1873">Coinage Act of 1873</a> stopped the coinage of silver for all coins worth a dollar or more, effectively tying the dollar to the value of gold. As a result, the <a href="/wiki/Deflation" title="Deflation">money supply contracted</a> and the effects of the Panic of 1873 grew worse, making it more expensive for debtors to pay debts they had contracted when currency was less valuable.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom356_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom356-162"><span>[</span>156<span>]</span></a></sup> Farmers and laborers, especially, clamored for the return of coinage in both metals, believing the increased money supply would restore wages and property values.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUnger358_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUnger358-163"><span>[</span>157<span>]</span></a></sup> Democratic Representative <a href="/wiki/Richard_P._Bland" title="Richard P. Bland">Richard P. Bland</a> of <a href="/wiki/Missouri" title="Missouri">Missouri</a> proposed a bill that would require the United States to coin as much silver as miners could sell the government, thus increasing the money supply and aiding debtors.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavison176.E2.80.93177_164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavison176.E2.80.93177-164"><span>[</span>158<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/William_B._Allison" title="William B. Allison">William B. Allison</a>, a Republican from <a href="/wiki/Iowa" title="Iowa">Iowa</a> offered an amendment in the Senate limiting the coinage to two to four million dollars per month, and the resulting <a href="/wiki/Bland%E2%80%93Allison_Act" title="Bland–Allison Act">Bland–Allison Act</a> passed both houses of Congress in 1878.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavison176.E2.80.93177_164-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavison176.E2.80.93177-164"><span>[</span>158<span>]</span></a></sup> Hayes feared that the Act would cause <a href="/wiki/Inflation" title="Inflation">inflation</a> that would be ruinous to business, effectively impairing contracts that were based on the gold dollar, as the silver dollar proposed in the bill would have an intrinsic value of 90 to 92 percent of the existing gold dollar.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom358.E2.80.93360_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom358.E2.80.93360-165"><span>[</span>159<span>]</span></a></sup> Further, Hayes believed that inflating the currency was an act of dishonesty, saying "[e]xpediency and justice both demand an honest currency."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom358.E2.80.93360_165-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom358.E2.80.93360-165"><span>[</span>159<span>]</span></a></sup> He vetoed the bill, but Congress overrode his veto, the only time it did so during his presidency.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavison176.E2.80.93177_164-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavison176.E2.80.93177-164"><span>[</span>158<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The second issue concerned <a href="/wiki/United_States_Notes" title="United States Notes" class="mw-redirect">United States Notes</a> (commonly called <a href="/wiki/Greenback_(money)" title="Greenback (money)" class="mw-redirect">greenbacks</a>), a form of <a href="/wiki/Fiat_money" title="Fiat money">fiat currency</a> first issued during the Civil War. The government accepted these notes as valid for payment of taxes and tariffs, but unlike ordinary dollars, they were not redeemable in gold.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavison176.E2.80.93177_164-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavison176.E2.80.93177-164"><span>[</span>158<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Specie_Payment_Resumption_Act" title="Specie Payment Resumption Act">Specie Payment Resumption Act</a> of 1875 required the treasury to redeem any outstanding greenbacks in gold, thus retiring them from circulation and restoring a single, gold-backed currency.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavison176.E2.80.93177_164-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavison176.E2.80.93177-164"><span>[</span>158<span>]</span></a></sup> Sherman agreed with Hayes's favorable opinion of the Act, and stockpiled gold in preparation for the exchange of greenbacks for gold.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom358.E2.80.93360_165-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom358.E2.80.93360-165"><span>[</span>159<span>]</span></a></sup> Once the public was confident that they could redeem greenbacks for specie (gold), however, few did so; when the Act took effect in 1879, only $130,000 out of the $346,000,000 outstanding dollars in greenbacks were actually redeemed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrefousse107_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrefousse107-166"><span>[</span>160<span>]</span></a></sup> Together with the Bland–Allison Act, the successful specie resumption effected a workable compromise between inflationists and <a href="/wiki/Hard_money_(policy)" title="Hard money (policy)">hard money</a> men and, as the world economy began to improve, agitation for more greenbacks and silver coinage quieted down for the rest of Hayes's term in office.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavison177.E2.80.93180_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavison177.E2.80.93180-167"><span>[</span>161<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_policy">Foreign policy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rutherford_B._Hayes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Foreign policy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_only_one_barred_out_cph.3b48680.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A Chinese man sitting outside a locked gate" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/The_only_one_barred_out_cph.3b48680.jpg/220px-The_only_one_barred_out_cph.3b48680.jpg" width="220" height="239" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/The_only_one_barred_out_cph.3b48680.jpg/330px-The_only_one_barred_out_cph.3b48680.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/The_only_one_barred_out_cph.3b48680.jpg/440px-The_only_one_barred_out_cph.3b48680.jpg 2x" data-file-width="976" data-file-height="1060" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_only_one_barred_out_cph.3b48680.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A political cartoon from 1882, criticizing Chinese exclusion</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Most of Hayes's foreign policy concerns involved <a href="/wiki/Latin_America" title="Latin America">Latin America</a>. In 1878, following the <a href="/wiki/Paraguayan_War" title="Paraguayan War">Paraguayan War</a>, he arbitrated a territorial dispute between <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a> and <a href="/wiki/Paraguay" title="Paraguay">Paraguay</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom416_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom416-168"><span>[</span>162<span>]</span></a></sup> Hayes awarded the disputed land in the <a href="/wiki/Gran_Chaco" title="Gran Chaco">Gran Chaco</a> region to Paraguay, and the Paraguayans honored him by renaming a city (<a href="/wiki/Villa_Hayes" title="Villa Hayes">Villa Hayes</a>) and a <a href="/wiki/Department_(country_subdivision)" title="Department (country subdivision)">department</a> (<a href="/wiki/Presidente_Hayes_Department" title="Presidente Hayes Department">Presidente Hayes</a>) in his honor.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom416_168-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom416-168"><span>[</span>162<span>]</span></a></sup> Hayes was also perturbed over the plans of <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_de_Lesseps" title="Ferdinand de Lesseps">Ferdinand de Lesseps</a>, the builder of the <a href="/wiki/Suez_Canal" title="Suez Canal">Suez Canal</a>, to construct a canal across the <a href="/wiki/Isthmus_of_Panama" title="Isthmus of Panama">Isthmus of Panama</a>, which was then owned by <a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">Colombia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom417.E2.80.93418_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom417.E2.80.93418-169"><span>[</span>163<span>]</span></a></sup> Concerned about a repetition of <a href="/wiki/French_intervention_in_Mexico" title="French intervention in Mexico">French adventurism in Mexico</a>, Hayes interpreted the <a href="/wiki/Monroe_Doctrine" title="Monroe Doctrine">Monroe Doctrine</a> firmly.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom420.E2.80.93421Barnard442_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom420.E2.80.93421Barnard442-170"><span>[</span>164<span>]</span></a></sup> In a message to Congress, Hayes explained his opinion on the canal: "The policy of this country is a canal under American control&#160;... The United States cannot consent to the surrender of this control to any European power or any combination of European powers."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom420.E2.80.93421Barnard442_170-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom420.E2.80.93421Barnard442-170"><span>[</span>164<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Mexico_%E2%80%93_United_States_border" title="Mexico – United States border" class="mw-redirect">Mexican border</a> also drew Hayes's attention. Throughout the 1870s, "lawless bands" often crossed the border on raids into Texas.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom335Barnard443_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom335Barnard443-171"><span>[</span>165<span>]</span></a></sup> Three months after taking office, Hayes granted the Army the power to pursue <a href="/wiki/Bandit" title="Bandit" class="mw-redirect">bandits</a>, even if it required crossing into Mexican territory.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom335Barnard443_171-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom335Barnard443-171"><span>[</span>165<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Porfirio_D%C3%ADaz" title="Porfirio Díaz">Porfirio Díaz</a>, the Mexican president, protested the order and sent troops to the border.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom335Barnard443_171-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom335Barnard443-171"><span>[</span>165<span>]</span></a></sup> The situation calmed as Díaz and Hayes agreed to jointly pursue bandits and Hayes agreed not to allow Mexican revolutionaries to raise armies in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom337Barnard444_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom337Barnard444-172"><span>[</span>166<span>]</span></a></sup> The violence along the border decreased, and in 1880 Hayes revoked the order allowing pursuit into Mexico.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom338_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom338-173"><span>[</span>167<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Outside of the Western hemisphere, Hayes's biggest foreign policy concern dealt with <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>. In 1868, the Senate had ratified the <a href="/wiki/Burlingame_Treaty" title="Burlingame Treaty">Burlingame Treaty</a> with China, allowing an unrestricted flow of <a href="/wiki/Chinese_American_history" title="Chinese American history" class="mw-redirect">Chinese immigrants</a> into the country. As the economy soured after the <a href="/wiki/Panic_of_1873" title="Panic of 1873">Panic of 1873</a>, Chinese immigrants were blamed for depressing workmen's wages.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom387_174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom387-174"><span>[</span>168<span>]</span></a></sup> During the Great Railroad Strike of 1877, anti-Chinese riots broke out in <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco" title="San Francisco">San Francisco</a>, and a <a href="/wiki/Third_party_(United_States)" title="Third party (United States)">third party</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Workingman%27s_Party" title="Workingman's Party" class="mw-redirect">Workingman's Party</a>, was formed with an emphasis on stopping Chinese immigration.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom387_174-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom387-174"><span>[</span>168<span>]</span></a></sup> In response, Congress passed a Chinese Exclusion Act in 1879, abrogating the 1868 treaty.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom388.E2.80.93389Barnard447.E2.80.93449_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom388.E2.80.93389Barnard447.E2.80.93449-175"><span>[</span>169<span>]</span></a></sup> Hayes vetoed the bill, believing that the United States should not abrogate treaties without negotiation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom388.E2.80.93389Barnard447.E2.80.93449_175-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom388.E2.80.93389Barnard447.E2.80.93449-175"><span>[</span>169<span>]</span></a></sup> The veto drew praise among eastern liberals, but Hayes was bitterly denounced in the West.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom388.E2.80.93389Barnard447.E2.80.93449_175-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom388.E2.80.93389Barnard447.E2.80.93449-175"><span>[</span>169<span>]</span></a></sup> In the subsequent furor, Democrats in the House of Representatives attempted to <a href="/wiki/Impeachment_in_the_United_States" title="Impeachment in the United States">impeach</a> him, but narrowly failed when Republicans prevented a <a href="/wiki/Quorum" title="Quorum">quorum</a> by refusing to vote.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom390.E2.80.93391_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom390.E2.80.93391-176"><span>[</span>170<span>]</span></a></sup> After the veto, <a href="/wiki/United_States_Assistant_Secretary_of_State" title="United States Assistant Secretary of State">Assistant Secretary of State</a> <a href="/wiki/Frederick_W._Seward" title="Frederick W. Seward">Frederick W. Seward</a> suggested that both countries work together to reduce immigration, and he and <a href="/wiki/James_Burrill_Angell" title="James Burrill Angell">James Burrill Angell</a> negotiated with the Chinese to do so.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom390.E2.80.93391_176-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom390.E2.80.93391-176"><span>[</span>170<span>]</span></a></sup> Congress passed a new law to that effect, the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Exclusion_Act" title="Chinese Exclusion Act">Chinese Exclusion Act</a> of 1882, after Hayes left office.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom390.E2.80.93391_176-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom390.E2.80.93391-176"><span>[</span>170<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Indian_policy">Indian policy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rutherford_B._Hayes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Indian policy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Schurz_Departing_Interior_Department.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Schurz_Departing_Interior_Department.jpg/220px-Schurz_Departing_Interior_Department.jpg" width="220" height="256" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Schurz_Departing_Interior_Department.jpg/330px-Schurz_Departing_Interior_Department.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Schurz_Departing_Interior_Department.jpg/440px-Schurz_Departing_Interior_Department.jpg 2x" data-file-width="900" data-file-height="1049" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Schurz_Departing_Interior_Department.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An 1881 political cartoon about <a href="/wiki/Carl_Schurz" title="Carl Schurz">Carl Schurz</a>'s management of the Indian Bureau</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Interior Secretary <a href="/wiki/Carl_Schurz" title="Carl Schurz">Carl Schurz</a> carried out Hayes's <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous peoples of the Americas">American Indian</a> policy, beginning with preventing the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_War" title="United States Department of War">War Department</a> from taking over the <a href="/wiki/Bureau_of_Indian_Affairs" title="Bureau of Indian Affairs">Bureau of Indian Affairs</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavison184.E2.80.93185_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavison184.E2.80.93185-177"><span>[</span>171<span>]</span></a></sup> Hayes and Schurz carried out a policy that included assimilation into white culture, educational training, and dividing Indian land into individual household allotments.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrefousse109Davison186.E2.80.93187_178-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrefousse109Davison186.E2.80.93187-178"><span>[</span>172<span>]</span></a></sup> Hayes believed that his policies would lead to self-sufficiency and peace between Indians and whites.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom341.E2.80.93343.2C_449.E2.80.93450_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom341.E2.80.93343.2C_449.E2.80.93450-179"><span>[</span>173<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Dawes_Act" title="Dawes Act">allotment system</a> was favored by liberal reformers at the time, including Schurz, but instead proved detrimental to American Indians. They lost much of their land through later sales to unscrupulous white <a href="/wiki/Speculation" title="Speculation">speculators</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStuart452.E2.80.93454_180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStuart452.E2.80.93454-180"><span>[</span>174<span>]</span></a></sup> Hayes and Schurz reformed the <a href="/wiki/Bureau_of_Indian_Affairs" title="Bureau of Indian Affairs">Bureau of Indian Affairs</a> to reduce fraud and gave Indians responsibility for policing their understaffed reservations.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom343.E2.80.93344.2C_449_181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom343.E2.80.93344.2C_449-181"><span>[</span>175<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Hayes dealt with several conflicts with Indian tribes. The <a href="/wiki/Nez_Perce_people" title="Nez Perce people">Nez Perce</a>, led by <a href="/wiki/Chief_Joseph" title="Chief Joseph">Chief Joseph</a>, began <a href="/wiki/Nez_Perce_War" title="Nez Perce War">an uprising</a> in June 1877 when Major General <a href="/wiki/Oliver_O._Howard" title="Oliver O. Howard">Oliver O. Howard</a> ordered them to move on to a <a href="/wiki/Indian_reservation" title="Indian reservation">reservation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom338.E2.80.93340_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom338.E2.80.93340-182"><span>[</span>176<span>]</span></a></sup> Howard's men defeated the Nez Perce in battle, and the tribe began a 1700-mile retreat into <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom338.E2.80.93340_182-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom338.E2.80.93340-182"><span>[</span>176<span>]</span></a></sup> In October, after a decisive battle at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Bear_Paw" title="Battle of Bear Paw">Bear Paw</a>, <a href="/wiki/Montana" title="Montana">Montana</a>, Chief Joseph surrendered and General <a href="/wiki/William_Tecumseh_Sherman" title="William Tecumseh Sherman">William T. Sherman</a> ordered the tribe transported to <a href="/wiki/Kansas" title="Kansas">Kansas</a>, where they were forced to remain until 1885.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom340.E2.80.93341_183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom340.E2.80.93341-183"><span>[</span>177<span>]</span></a></sup> The Nez Perce war was not the last conflict in the West, as the <a href="/wiki/Bannock_people" title="Bannock people">Bannock</a> <a href="/wiki/Bannock_War" title="Bannock War">rose up</a> in Spring 1878 and raided nearby settlements before being defeated by Howard's army in July of that year.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavison184.E2.80.93185_177-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavison184.E2.80.93185-177"><span>[</span>171<span>]</span></a></sup> War with the <a href="/wiki/Ute_people" title="Ute people">Ute tribe</a> broke out in 1879 when the Utes killed Indian agent <a href="/wiki/Nathan_Meeker" title="Nathan Meeker">Nathan Meeker</a>, who had been attempting to convert them to Christianity.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrefousse123_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrefousse123-184"><span>[</span>178<span>]</span></a></sup> The subsequent <a href="/wiki/White_River_War" title="White River War">White River War</a> ended when Schurz negotiated peace with the Ute and prevented the white Coloradans from taking revenge for Meeker's death.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrefousse123_184-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrefousse123-184"><span>[</span>178<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Hayes also became involved in resolving the removal of the <a href="/wiki/Ponca" title="Ponca">Ponca</a> tribe from <a href="/wiki/Nebraska" title="Nebraska">Nebraska</a> to <a href="/wiki/Indian_Territory" title="Indian Territory">Indian Territory</a> (present-day <a href="/wiki/Oklahoma" title="Oklahoma">Oklahoma</a>) because of a misunderstanding during the Grant Administration.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom450.E2.80.93454Sproat173_185-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom450.E2.80.93454Sproat173-185"><span>[</span>179<span>]</span></a></sup> The tribe's problems came to Hayes's attention after their chief, <a href="/wiki/Standing_Bear" title="Standing Bear">Standing Bear</a>, filed a lawsuit to contest Schurz's demand that they stay in Indian Territory. Overruling Schurz, Hayes set up a commission in 1880 that ruled Ponca were free to return to Nebraska or stay on their reservation in Indian Territory.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom450.E2.80.93454Sproat173_185-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom450.E2.80.93454Sproat173-185"><span>[</span>179<span>]</span></a></sup> The Ponca were awarded compensation for their land rights, which had been previously granted to the <a href="/wiki/Sioux" title="Sioux">Sioux</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom450.E2.80.93454Sproat173_185-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom450.E2.80.93454Sproat173-185"><span>[</span>179<span>]</span></a></sup> In a message to Congress in February 1881, Hayes insisted he would "give to these injured people that measure of redress which is required alike by justice and by humanity."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrefousse124_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrefousse124-186"><span>[</span>180<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hayes.27s_White_House">Hayes's White House</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rutherford_B._Hayes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Hayes's White House">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Hayes and his wife <a href="/wiki/Lucy_Webb_Hayes" title="Lucy Webb Hayes">Lucy</a> were known for their policy of keeping an alcohol-free White House, giving rise to her nickname "Lemonade Lucy."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom3Davisonxv_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom3Davisonxv-187"><span>[</span>181<span>]</span></a></sup> The first reception at the Hayes White House included <a href="/wiki/Wine" title="Wine">wine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavison82Barnard480_188-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavison82Barnard480-188"><span>[</span>182<span>]</span></a></sup> However, Hayes was dismayed at drunken behavior at receptions hosted by ambassadors around Washington, leading him to follow his wife's <a href="/wiki/Temperance_movement" title="Temperance movement">temperance</a> leanings.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom384_189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom384-189"><span>[</span>183<span>]</span></a></sup> Alcohol was not served again in the Hayes White House. Critics charged Hayes with parsimony, but Hayes spent more money (which came out of his personal budget) after the ban, ordering that any savings from eliminating alcohol be used on more lavish entertainment.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom385.E2.80.93386Barnard480_190-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom385.E2.80.93386Barnard480-190"><span>[</span>184<span>]</span></a></sup> His temperance policy also paid political dividends, strengthening his support among Protestant ministers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom384_189-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom384-189"><span>[</span>183<span>]</span></a></sup> Although Secretary Evarts quipped that at the White House dinners, "water flowed like wine," the policy was a success in convincing <a href="/wiki/Prohibition_in_the_United_States" title="Prohibition in the United States">prohibitionists</a> to vote Republican.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom458_191-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom458-191"><span>[</span>185<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Administration_and_Cabinet">Administration and Cabinet</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rutherford_B._Hayes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Administration and Cabinet">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hayes_cabinet_2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Several men sitting around a table" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Hayes_cabinet_2.jpg/400px-Hayes_cabinet_2.jpg" width="400" height="297" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Hayes_cabinet_2.jpg/600px-Hayes_cabinet_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Hayes_cabinet_2.jpg/800px-Hayes_cabinet_2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1536" data-file-height="1140" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hayes_cabinet_2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Hayes's cabinet in 1877</div>
</div>
</div>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Judicial_appointments">Judicial appointments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rutherford_B._Hayes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Judicial appointments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_federal_judges_appointed_by_Rutherford_B._Hayes" title="List of federal judges appointed by Rutherford B. Hayes">List of federal judges appointed by Rutherford B. Hayes</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Thomas_Stanley_Matthews_-_Brady-Handy.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Black-and-white photograph of a bearded man" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Thomas_Stanley_Matthews_-_Brady-Handy.jpg/170px-Thomas_Stanley_Matthews_-_Brady-Handy.jpg" width="170" height="215" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Thomas_Stanley_Matthews_-_Brady-Handy.jpg/255px-Thomas_Stanley_Matthews_-_Brady-Handy.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Thomas_Stanley_Matthews_-_Brady-Handy.jpg/340px-Thomas_Stanley_Matthews_-_Brady-Handy.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2824" data-file-height="3568" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Thomas_Stanley_Matthews_-_Brady-Handy.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Stanley_Matthews_(lawyer)" title="Stanley Matthews (lawyer)">Stanley Matthews's</a> confirmation to the Supreme Court was more difficult than Hayes expected.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Hayes appointed two <a href="/wiki/Associate_Justice_of_the_Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Associate Justice of the Supreme Court of the United States">Associate Justices</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">Supreme Court</a>. The first vacancy occurred when David Davis resigned to enter the Senate during the election controversy of 1876. On taking office, Hayes appointed <a href="/wiki/John_Marshall_Harlan" title="John Marshall Harlan">John Marshall Harlan</a> to the seat. A former candidate for governor of Kentucky, Harlan had been <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Bristow" title="Benjamin Bristow">Benjamin Bristow</a>'s campaign manager at the 1876 Republican convention, and Hayes had earlier considered him for <a href="/wiki/Attorney_General_of_the_United_States" title="Attorney General of the United States" class="mw-redirect">Attorney General</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavison130.E2.80.93132_192-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavison130.E2.80.93132-192"><span>[</span>186<span>]</span></a></sup> Hayes submitted the nomination in October 1877, but it aroused some dissent in the Senate because of Harlan's limited experience in public office.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavison130.E2.80.93132_192-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavison130.E2.80.93132-192"><span>[</span>186<span>]</span></a></sup> Harlan was nonetheless confirmed and served on the court for thirty-four years, in which he voted (usually in the minority) for an aggressive enforcement of the civil rights laws.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavison130.E2.80.93132_192-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavison130.E2.80.93132-192"><span>[</span>186<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1880, a second seat became vacant upon the resignation of Justice <a href="/wiki/William_Strong_(judge)" title="William Strong (judge)" class="mw-redirect">William Strong</a>. Hayes nominated <a href="/wiki/William_Burnham_Woods" title="William Burnham Woods">William Burnham Woods</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Carpetbagger" title="Carpetbagger">carpetbagger</a> Republican <a href="/wiki/Circuit_court" title="Circuit court">circuit court</a> judge from <a href="/wiki/Alabama" title="Alabama">Alabama</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavison132Hoogenboom454_193-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavison132Hoogenboom454-193"><span>[</span>187<span>]</span></a></sup> Woods served six years on the Court, ultimately proving a disappointment to Hayes as he interpreted the Constitution in a manner more similar to that of Southern Democrats than to Hayes's own preferences.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarnard268.2C_498_194-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarnard268.2C_498-194"><span>[</span>188<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Hayes attempted, unsuccessfully, to fill a third vacancy in 1881. Justice <a href="/wiki/Noah_Haynes_Swayne" title="Noah Haynes Swayne">Noah Haynes Swayne</a> resigned with the expectation that Hayes would fill his seat by appointing <a href="/wiki/Stanley_Matthews_(lawyer)" title="Stanley Matthews (lawyer)">Stanley Matthews</a>, who was a friend of both men.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavison129_195-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavison129-195"><span>[</span>189<span>]</span></a></sup> Many Senators objected to the appointment, believing that Matthews was too close to corporate and railroad interests, especially those of <a href="/wiki/Jay_Gould" title="Jay Gould">Jay Gould</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarnard498.E2.80.93499_196-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarnard498.E2.80.93499-196"><span>[</span>190<span>]</span></a></sup> and the Senate adjourned without voting on the nomination.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavison129_195-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavison129-195"><span>[</span>189<span>]</span></a></sup> The following year, when <a href="/wiki/James_A._Garfield" title="James A. Garfield">James A. Garfield</a> entered the White House, he re-submitted Matthews's nomination to the Senate, which this time confirmed Matthews by one vote, 24 to 23.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavison129_195-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavison129-195"><span>[</span>189<span>]</span></a></sup> Matthews served for eight years until his death in 1889. His opinion in <i><a href="/wiki/Yick_Wo_v._Hopkins" title="Yick Wo v. Hopkins">Yick Wo v. Hopkins</a></i> in 1886 advanced his and Hayes' views on the protection of ethnic minorities' rights.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom457_197-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom457-197"><span>[</span>191<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_life_and_death">Later life and death</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rutherford_B._Hayes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Later life and death">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Daniel_Huntington_-_Rutherford_Birchard_Hayes_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Painting of a bearded man, standing" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Daniel_Huntington_-_Rutherford_Birchard_Hayes_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/170px-Daniel_Huntington_-_Rutherford_Birchard_Hayes_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" width="170" height="287" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Daniel_Huntington_-_Rutherford_Birchard_Hayes_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/255px-Daniel_Huntington_-_Rutherford_Birchard_Hayes_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Daniel_Huntington_-_Rutherford_Birchard_Hayes_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/340px-Daniel_Huntington_-_Rutherford_Birchard_Hayes_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3879" data-file-height="6544" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Daniel_Huntington_-_Rutherford_Birchard_Hayes_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
White House portrait of Rutherford B. Hayes</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Hayes declined to seek re-election in <a href="/wiki/U.S._presidential_election,_1880" title="U.S. presidential election, 1880" class="mw-redirect">1880</a>, keeping his pledge that he would not run for a second term. He was gratified with the election of fellow Ohio Republican <a href="/wiki/James_A._Garfield" title="James A. Garfield">James A. Garfield</a> to succeed him, and consulted with him on appointments for the next administration.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom447.E2.80.93465_198-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom447.E2.80.93465-198"><span>[</span>192<span>]</span></a></sup> After Garfield's inauguration, Hayes and his family returned to <a href="/wiki/Spiegel_Grove" title="Spiegel Grove">Spiegel Grove</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom466.E2.80.93467_199-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom466.E2.80.93467-199"><span>[</span>193<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1881, he was elected a companion of the <a href="/wiki/Military_Order_of_the_Loyal_Legion_of_the_United_States" title="Military Order of the Loyal Legion of the United States">Military Order of the Loyal Legion of the United States</a>. He served as commander-in-chief (national president) of the Loyal Legion from 1888 until his death in 1893. Although he remained a loyal Republican, Hayes was not too disappointed in <a href="/wiki/Grover_Cleveland" title="Grover Cleveland">Grover Cleveland</a>'s election to the Presidency in 1884, approving of the New York Democrat's views on civil service reform.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom483_200-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom483-200"><span>[</span>194<span>]</span></a></sup> He was also pleased at the progress of the political career of <a href="/wiki/William_McKinley" title="William McKinley">William McKinley</a>, his army comrade and political protégé.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom524.E2.80.93525_201-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom524.E2.80.93525-201"><span>[</span>195<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Hayes became an advocate for educational charities, advocating federal education subsidies for all children.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom471.E2.80.93475Thelen156_202-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom471.E2.80.93475Thelen156-202"><span>[</span>196<span>]</span></a></sup> He believed that education was the best way to heal the rifts in American society and allow individuals to improve themselves.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThelen154.E2.80.93156_203-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThelen154.E2.80.93156-203"><span>[</span>197<span>]</span></a></sup> Hayes was appointed to the Board of Trustees of <a href="/wiki/The_Ohio_State_University" title="The Ohio State University" class="mw-redirect">The Ohio State University</a>, the school he helped found during his time as governor of Ohio, in 1887.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom498.E2.80.93499_204-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom498.E2.80.93499-204"><span>[</span>198<span>]</span></a></sup> He emphasized the need for <a href="/wiki/Vocational_school" title="Vocational school">vocational</a>, as well as academic, education: "I preach the gospel of work," he wrote, "I believe in skilled labor as a part of education."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarnard506_205-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarnard506-205"><span>[</span>199<span>]</span></a></sup> He urged Congress, unsuccessfully, to pass a bill written by Senator <a href="/wiki/Henry_W._Blair" title="Henry W. Blair">Henry W. Blair</a> that would have allowed federal aid for education for the first time.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESwint48.E2.80.9349_206-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESwint48.E2.80.9349-206"><span>[</span>200<span>]</span></a></sup> Hayes gave a speech in 1889 encouraging black students to apply for scholarships from the <a href="/wiki/Slater_Fund" title="Slater Fund">Slater Fund</a>, one of the charities with which he was affiliated.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom518.E2.80.93523_207-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom518.E2.80.93523-207"><span>[</span>201<span>]</span></a></sup> One such student, <a href="/wiki/W._E._B._Du_Bois" title="W. E. B. Du Bois">W. E. B. Du Bois</a>, received a scholarship in 1892.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom518.E2.80.93523_207-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom518.E2.80.93523-207"><span>[</span>201<span>]</span></a></sup> Hayes also advocated <a href="/wiki/Prison_reform" title="Prison reform">better prison conditions</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom496.E2.80.93497Thelen151_208-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom496.E2.80.93497Thelen151-208"><span>[</span>202<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In retirement, Hayes was troubled by the disparity between the rich and the poor, saying in an 1886 speech that "free government cannot long endure if property is largely in a few hands and large masses of people are unable to earn homes, education, and a support in old age."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarnard513Hoogenboom539_209-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarnard513Hoogenboom539-209"><span>[</span>203<span>]</span></a></sup> The following year, Hayes recorded his thoughts on that subject in his diary:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>In church it occurred to me that it is time for the public to hear that the giant evil and danger in this country, the danger which transcends all others, is the vast wealth owned or controlled by a few persons. Money is power. In Congress, in state legislatures, in city councils, in the courts, in the political conventions, in the press, in the pulpit, in the circles of the educated and the talented, its influence is growing greater and greater. Excessive wealth in the hands of the few means extreme poverty, ignorance, vice, and wretchedness as the lot of the many. It is not yet time to debate about the remedy. The previous question is as to the danger—the evil. Let the people be fully informed and convinced as to the evil. Let them earnestly seek the remedy and it will be found. Fully to know the evil is the first step towards reaching its eradication. <a href="/wiki/Henry_George" title="Henry George">Henry George</a> is strong when he portrays the rottenness of the present system. We are, to say the least, not yet ready for his remedy. We may reach and remove the difficulty by changes in the laws regulating corporations, descents of property, wills, trusts, taxation, and a host of other important interests, not omitting lands and other property.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHayes354v._4Swint46.E2.80.9347_210-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHayes354v._4Swint46.E2.80.9347-210"><span>[</span>204<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Hayes was greatly saddened by his wife's death in 1889.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom508.E2.80.93510_211-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom508.E2.80.93510-211"><span>[</span>205<span>]</span></a></sup> He wrote that "the soul had left [Spiegel Grove]" when she died.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom508.E2.80.93510_211-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom508.E2.80.93510-211"><span>[</span>205<span>]</span></a></sup> After Lucy's death, Hayes's daughter Fanny became his traveling companion, and he enjoyed visits from his grandchildren.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom509.E2.80.93520_212-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom509.E2.80.93520-212"><span>[</span>206<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1890, he chaired the Lake Mohonk Conference on the Negro Question, a gathering of reformers that met in upstate New York to discuss racial issues.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom515.E2.80.93517Foner605.E2.80.93606_213-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom515.E2.80.93517Foner605.E2.80.93606-213"><span>[</span>207<span>]</span></a></sup> Hayes died of complications of a heart attack at his home on January 17, 1893.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarnard522.E2.80.93523_214-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarnard522.E2.80.93523-214"><span>[</span>208<span>]</span></a></sup> His last words were "I know that I'm going where Lucy is."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarnard522.E2.80.93523_214-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarnard522.E2.80.93523-214"><span>[</span>208<span>]</span></a></sup> President-elect Grover Cleveland and Ohio Governor William McKinley led the funeral procession that followed Hayes's body until he was interred in <a href="/wiki/Oakwood_Cemetery_(Fremont,_Ohio)" title="Oakwood Cemetery (Fremont, Ohio)">Oakwood Cemetery</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom532.E2.80.93533_215-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom532.E2.80.93533-215"><span>[</span>209<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy_and_honors">Legacy and honors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rutherford_B._Hayes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Legacy and honors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width: 210px;">
<div style="width: 210px; height: 270px; overflow: hidden;">
<div style="position: relative; top: -50px; left: -50px; width: 310px"><a href="/wiki/File:HAYES,_Rutherford_B-President_(BEP_engraved_portrait).jpg" class="image"><img alt="BEP engraved portrait of Hayes as President." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/HAYES%2C_Rutherford_B-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg/310px-HAYES%2C_Rutherford_B-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg" width="310" height="408" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/HAYES%2C_Rutherford_B-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg/465px-HAYES%2C_Rutherford_B-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/HAYES%2C_Rutherford_B-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg/620px-HAYES%2C_Rutherford_B-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="8208" data-file-height="10792" /></a></div>
</div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HAYES,_Rutherford_B-President_(BEP_engraved_portrait).jpg" title="File:HAYES, Rutherford B-President (BEP engraved portrait).jpg">File:HAYES, Rutherford B-President (BEP engraved portrait).jpg</a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Bureau_of_Engraving_and_Printing" title="Bureau of Engraving and Printing">BEP</a> engraved portrait of Hayes as President.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Following the donation of his home to the state of Ohio for the <a href="/wiki/Spiegel_Grove" title="Spiegel Grove">Spiegel Grove State Park</a>, he was re-interred there in 1915.<sup id="cite_ref-NPS_2004-01-24_216-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NPS_2004-01-24-216"><span>[</span>210<span>]</span></a></sup> The following year the <a href="/wiki/Rutherford_B._Hayes_Presidential_Center" title="Rutherford B. Hayes Presidential Center">Hayes Commemorative Library and Museum</a>, the first presidential library in the United States, was opened on the site, funded by contributions from the state of Ohio and Hayes' family.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith485.E2.80.93488_217-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith485.E2.80.93488-217"><span>[</span>211<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>An 1878 dispute between Argentina and Paraguay which Hayes had arbitrated and decided in favor of Paraguay, giving Paraguay 60 percent of its current territory later motivated a province in the region to be named after him: <a href="/wiki/Presidente_Hayes_Department" title="Presidente Hayes Department">Presidente Hayes province</a> (capital: <a href="/wiki/Villa_Hayes" title="Villa Hayes">Villa Hayes</a>); an official holiday: Nov. 12, the anniversary of the decision, celebrated in Presidente Hayes province; a local soccer team: <a href="/wiki/Club_Presidente_Hayes" title="Club Presidente Hayes">Presidente Hayes soccer club</a>, based in the national capital, <a href="/wiki/Asuncion" title="Asuncion" class="mw-redirect">Asuncion</a> (also known as "Los Yanquis"); a postage stamp the design of which was chosen in a contest run by the U.S. Embassy; and even a young girl's wish: a girl who came out of a coma got her fondest wish — a trip to the Hayes Presidential Center in Fremont, Ohio.<sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218"><span>[</span>212<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rutherford_B._Hayes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: lower-alpha;">

<li id="cite_note-25"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-25">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Herron's daughter, <a href="/wiki/Helen_Herron_Taft" title="Helen Herron Taft">Helen</a>, later married <a href="/wiki/William_Howard_Taft" title="William Howard Taft">William Howard Taft</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarnard167_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarnard167-24"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-82"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-82">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">his first two sons, Joseph and George, had died in infancy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrefousse31.2C_42_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrefousse31.2C_42-81"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a></sup></span></li>

Watts (page does not exist)">John W. Watts</a>, was disqualified because he held "an Office of Trust or Profit under the United States", in violation of <a href="/wiki/Article_Two_of_the_United_States_Constitution#Clause_2:_Method_of_choosing_electors" title="Article Two of the United States Constitution">Article II, section 1, clause 2</a> of the U.S. Constitution.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom279_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoogenboom279-108"><span>[</span>105<span>]</span></a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-128"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-128">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">At the time of the 1876 election there were only three states, Florida, South Carolina, and Louisiana, in which the Reconstruction regime still survived. In Florida the Reconstructionists lost control as the Democrats won the election there, leaving South Carolina and Louisiana as the only states in which the regime was supported by Federal troops.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrefousse90.E2.80.9391_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrefousse90.E2.80.9391-127"><span>[</span>123<span>]</span></a></sup></span></li>

</ol>
</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rutherford_B._Hayes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

Hayes and the Reform Tradition in the Gilded Age". <i>American Quarterly</i> <b>22</b> (2): 150–165. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//dx.doi.org/10.2307%2F2711639">10.2307/2711639</a>. <a href="/wiki/JSTOR" title="JSTOR">JSTOR</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.jstor.org/stable/2711639">2711639</a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ARutherford+B.+Hayes&amp;rft.atitle=Rutherford+B.+Hayes+and+the+Reform+Tradition+in+the+Gilded+Age&amp;rft.aufirst=David+P.&amp;rft.aulast=Thelen&amp;rft.date=Summer+1970&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.2307%2F2711639&amp;rft.issue=2&amp;rft.jstor=2711639&amp;rft.jtitle=American+Quarterly&amp;rft.pages=150-165&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.volume=22" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></li>
